<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Lung</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Primary organ of the respiratory system</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lung_(disambiguation)" title="Lung (disambiguation)">Lung (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article uses <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">anatomical terminology</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output <p>The <b>lungs</b> are the primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a> in <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> and most other <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, including some <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a> and a small number of <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and most other <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, two lungs are located near the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">backbone</a> on either side of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>. Their function in the respiratory system is to extract <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> and transfer it into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloodstream" title="Bloodstream">bloodstream</a>, and to release <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the bloodstream into the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a>, in a process of <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a>. <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">Respiration</a> is driven by different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscular systems</a> in different species. Mammals, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> use their different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a> to support and foster <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">breathing</a>. In earlier <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, air was driven into the lungs by the <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_muscles" title="Pharyngeal muscles">pharyngeal muscles</a> via <a href="/wiki/Buccal_pumping" title="Buccal pumping">buccal pumping</a>, a mechanism still seen in <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>. In humans, the main <a href="/wiki/Muscles_of_respiration" title="Muscles of respiration">muscle of respiration</a> that drives breathing is the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>. The lungs also provide airflow that makes vocal sounds including human <a href="/wiki/Speech" title="Speech">speech</a> possible.
</p><p>Humans have two lungs, a right lung and a left lung.  They are situated within the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_cavity" title="Thoracic cavity">thoracic cavity</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest" title="Chest">chest</a>. The right lung is bigger than the left, which shares space in the chest with the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>. The lungs together weigh approximately 1.3 kilograms (2.9 lb), and the right is heavier. The lungs are part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_respiratory_tract" title="Lower respiratory tract">lower respiratory tract</a> that begins at the <a href="/wiki/Trachea" title="Trachea">trachea</a> and branches into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronchi" title="Bronchi">bronchi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronchiole" title="Bronchiole">bronchioles</a>, and which receive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> <a href="/wiki/Inhalation" title="Inhalation">breathed in</a> via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conducting_zone" title="Conducting zone">conducting zone</a>. The conducting zone ends at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_bronchioles" title="Terminal bronchioles">terminal bronchioles</a>. These divide into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_bronchioles" title="Respiratory bronchioles">respiratory bronchioles</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract#Structure" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory zone</a> which divide into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_duct" title="Alveolar duct">alveolar ducts</a> that give rise to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_sac" title="Alveolar sac">alveolar sacs</a> that contain the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a>, where gas exchange takes place. Alveoli are also sparsely present on the walls of the respiratory bronchioles and alveolar ducts. Together, the lungs contain approximately 2,400 kilometres (1,500 mi) of airways and 300 to 500 million alveoli. Each lung is enclosed within a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleural_sac" title="Pleural sac">pleural sac</a> of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serous_membranes" title="Serous membranes">membranes</a> called <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_pleurae" title="Pulmonary pleurae">pleurae</a>; the membranes are separated by a film of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleural_fluid" title="Pleural fluid">pleural fluid</a>, which allows the inner and outer membranes to slide over each other whilst <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">breathing</a> takes place, without much friction. The inner pleura also divides each lung into sections called <a href="/wiki/Lobe_(anatomy)" title="Lobe (anatomy)">lobes</a>. The right lung has three lobes and the left has two. The lobes are further divided into  <a href="/wiki/Bronchopulmonary_segment" title="Bronchopulmonary segment">bronchopulmonary segments</a> and  <a href="#Microanatomy">pulmonary lobules</a>. The lungs have a unique blood supply, receiving deoxygenated blood from the heart in the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a> for the purposes of receiving oxygen and releasing carbon dioxide, and a separate supply of oxygenated blood to the tissue of the lungs, in the <a href="/wiki/Bronchial_circulation" title="Bronchial circulation">bronchial circulation</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Parenchyma#Lung_parenchyma" title="Parenchyma">tissue of the lungs</a> can be affected by a number of <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_disease" title="Respiratory disease">respiratory diseases</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> includes <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis#Chronic_bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">chronic bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a>, and can be related to <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a> or exposure to <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">harmful substances</a>.  A number of <a href="/wiki/Occupational_lung_disease" title="Occupational lung disease">occupational lung diseases</a> can be caused by substances such as <a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos fibres</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystalline</a> <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silica</a> dust. Diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> can also affect the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a>. Medical terms related to the lung often begin with <i>pulmo-</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>pulmonarius</i> (of the lungs) as in <a href="/wiki/Pulmonology" title="Pulmonology">pulmonology</a>, or with <i>pneumo-</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> πνεύμων "lung") as in <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic development</a>, the lungs begin to develop as an outpouching of the <a href="/wiki/Foregut" title="Foregut">foregut</a>, a tube which goes on to form the upper part of the <a href="/wiki/Human_digestive_system" title="Human digestive system">digestive system</a>. When the lungs are formed the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> is held in the <a href="/wiki/Amniotic_fluid" title="Amniotic fluid">fluid-filled</a> <a href="/wiki/Amniotic_sac" title="Amniotic sac">amniotic sac</a> and so they do not function to breathe. Blood is also diverted from the lungs through the <a href="/wiki/Ductus_arteriosus" title="Ductus arteriosus">ductus arteriosus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_extrauterine_life#Breathing_and_circulation" title="Adaptation to extrauterine life">At birth</a> however, air begins to pass through the lungs, and the diversionary duct closes, so that the lungs can begin to respire. The lungs only fully develop in early childhood.
</p>

<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg"><img alt="Illu bronchi lungs.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg/330px-Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg/495px-Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illu_bronchi_lungs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>The lungs are located in the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_cavity" title="Thoracic cavity">chest</a> on either side of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a>. They are conical in shape with a narrow rounded <b>apex</b> at the top, and a broad concave <b>base</b> that rests on the convex surface of the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup> The apex of the lung extends into the root of the neck, reaching shortly above the level of the <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">sternal</a> end of the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage#Bones" title="Rib cage">first rib</a>. The lungs stretch from close to the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">backbone</a> in the rib cage to the front of the <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">chest</a> and downwards from the lower part of the trachea to the diaphragm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup> The left lung shares space with the heart, and has an indentation in its border called the <b>cardiac notch of the left lung</b> to accommodate this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betts_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Betts-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup> The front and outer sides of the lungs face the ribs, which make light indentations on their surfaces. The medial surfaces of the lungs face towards the centre of the chest, and lie against the heart, <a href="/wiki/Great_vessels" title="Great vessels">great vessels</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Carina_of_trachea" title="Carina of trachea">carina</a> where the trachea divides into the two main bronchi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup>  The <b>cardiac impression</b> is an indentation formed on the surfaces of the lungs where they rest against the heart.
</p><p>Both lungs have a central recession called the <a href="/wiki/Root_of_the_lung" title="Root of the lung">hilum</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">airways</a> pass into the lungs making up the <a href="/wiki/Root_of_the_lung" title="Root of the lung">root of the lung</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_K_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_K-4">[4]</a></sup> There are also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracheobronchial_lymph_node" title="Tracheobronchial lymph node">bronchopulmonary lymph nodes</a> on the hilum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lungs are surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_pleurae" title="Pulmonary pleurae">pulmonary pleurae</a>. The pleurae are two <a href="/wiki/Serous_membrane" title="Serous membrane">serous membranes</a>; the outer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parietal_pleura" title="Parietal pleura">parietal pleura</a> lines the inner wall of the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a> and the inner <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visceral_pleura" title="Visceral pleura">visceral pleura</a> directly lines the surface of the lungs. Between the pleurae is a <a href="/wiki/Potential_space" title="Potential space">potential space</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Pleural_cavity" title="Pleural cavity">pleural cavity</a> containing a thin layer of lubricating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleural_fluid" title="Pleural fluid">pleural fluid</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobes">Lobes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lobes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;margin-left:10px">
<caption>Lobes and bronchopulmonary segments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Right lung</th>
<th>Left lung
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Upper</b>
<ul><li>Apical</li>
<li>Posterior</li>
<li>Anterior</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Middle</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Lateral</li>
<li>Medial</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Lower</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Superior</li>
<li>Medial</li>
<li>Anterior</li>
<li>Lateral</li>
<li>Posterior</li></ul>
</td>
<td><b>Upper</b>
<ul><li>Apicoposterior</li>
<li>Anterior</li></ul>
<p><b>Lingula</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Superior</li>
<li>Inferior</li></ul>
<p><b>Lower</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Superior</li>
<li>Anteriomedial</li>
<li>Lateral</li>
<li>Posterior</li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Each lung is divided into sections called lobes by the infoldings of the visceral pleura as fissures. Lobes are divided into segments, and segments have further divisions as lobules. There are three lobes in the right lung and two lobes in the left lung.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fissures">Fissures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fissures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The fissures are formed in early <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">prenatal development</a> by invaginations of the visceral pleura that divide the lobar bronchi, and section the lungs into lobes that helps in their expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koster_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koster-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lung_fissures_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lung_fissures-7">[7]</a></sup> The right lung is divided into three lobes by a <b>horizontal fissure</b>, and an <b>oblique fissure</b>. The left lung is divided into two lobes by an oblique fissure which is closely aligned with the oblique fissure in the right lung. In the right lung the upper horizontal fissure, separates the upper (superior) lobe from the middle lobe. The lower, oblique fissure separates the lower lobe from the middle and upper lobes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lung_fissures_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lung_fissures-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lung#Variation" title="Lung">Variations</a> in the fissures are fairly common being either incompletely formed 
or present as an extra fissure as in the <a href="/wiki/Azygos_lobe" title="Azygos lobe">azygos fissure</a>, or absent. Incomplete fissures are responsible for <a href="/wiki/Collateral_ventilation" title="Collateral ventilation">interlobar collateral ventilation</a>, airflow between lobes which is unwanted in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lung_volume_reduction" title="Lung volume reduction">lung volume reduction</a> procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koster_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koster-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Segments">Segments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Segments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The main or primary bronchi enter the lungs at the hilum and initially branch into <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">secondary bronchi</a> also known as lobar bronchi that supply air to each lobe of the lung. The lobar bronchi branch into <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">tertiary bronchi</a> also known as segmental bronchi and these supply air to the further divisions of the lobes known as <a href="/wiki/Bronchopulmonary_segment" title="Bronchopulmonary segment">bronchopulmonary segments</a>. Each bronchopulmonary segment has its own (segmental) bronchus and <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arterial supply</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Segments for the left and right lung are shown in the table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1-5">[5]</a></sup> The segmental anatomy is useful clinically for localising disease processes in the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1-5">[5]</a></sup> A segment is a discrete unit that can be surgically removed without seriously affecting surrounding tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti (left) and right lung (right). The lobes of the lungs can be seen, and the central <a href="/wiki/Root_of_the_lung" title="Root of the lung">root of the lung</a> is also present.</div></div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_lung">Right lung</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Right lung">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The right lung has both more lobes and segments than the left. It is divided into three lobes, an upper, middle, and a lower lobe by two fissures, one oblique and one horizontal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaudhry_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaudhry-10">[10]</a></sup> The upper, horizontal fissure, separates the upper from the middle lobe. It begins in the lower oblique fissure near the posterior border of the lung, and, running horizontally forward, cuts the anterior border on a level with the <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">sternal</a> end of the fourth <a href="/wiki/Costal_cartilage" title="Costal cartilage">costal cartilage</a>; on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediastinal" title="Mediastinal">mediastinal</a> surface it may be traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Root_of_the_lung" title="Root of the lung">hilum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup> The lower, oblique fissure, separates the lower from the middle and upper lobes and is closely aligned with the oblique fissure in the left lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lung_fissures_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lung_fissures-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mediastinal surface of the right lung is indented by a number of nearby structures. The heart sits in an impression called the cardiac impression. Above the hilum of the lung is an arched groove for the <a href="/wiki/Azygos_vein" title="Azygos vein">azygos vein</a>, and above this is a wide groove for the <a href="/wiki/Superior_vena_cava" title="Superior vena cava">superior vena cava</a> and right <a href="/wiki/Brachiocephalic_vein" title="Brachiocephalic vein">brachiocephalic vein</a>; behind this, and close to the top of the lung is a groove for the <a href="/wiki/Brachiocephalic_artery" title="Brachiocephalic artery">brachiocephalic artery</a>. There is a groove for the esophagus behind the hilum and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_ligament" title="Pulmonary ligament">pulmonary ligament</a>, and near the lower part of the esophageal groove is a deeper groove for the <a href="/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava" title="Inferior vena cava">inferior vena cava</a> before it enters the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The weight of the right lung varies between individuals, with a standard <a href="/wiki/Reference_range" title="Reference range">reference range</a> in men of 155–720 g (0.342–1.587 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaDiMaio2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaDiMaio2012-11">[11]</a></sup> and in women of 100–590 g (0.22–1.30 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaDiMaio2015_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaDiMaio2015-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Left_lung">Left lung</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Left lung">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The left lung is divided into two lobes, an upper and a lower lobe, by the oblique fissure, which extends from the <a href="/wiki/Rib" title="Rib">costal</a> to the mediastinal surface of the lung both above and below the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilum_of_lung" title="Hilum of lung">hilum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup> The left lung, unlike the right, does not have a middle lobe, though it does have a <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> feature, a projection of the upper lobe termed the <b>lingula</b>.  Its name means "little tongue". The lingula on the left lung serves as an anatomic parallel to the middle lobe on the right lung, with both areas being predisposed to similar infections and anatomic complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> There are two <a href="/wiki/Bronchopulmonary_segment" title="Bronchopulmonary segment">bronchopulmonary segments</a> of the lingula: superior and inferior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mediastinal surface of the left lung has a large <i>cardiac impression</i> where the heart sits. This is deeper and larger than that on the right lung, at which level the heart projects to the left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the same surface, immediately above the hilum, is a well-marked curved groove for  the <a href="/wiki/Aortic_arch" title="Aortic arch">aortic arch</a>, and a groove below it for the <a href="/wiki/Descending_aorta" title="Descending aorta">descending aorta</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Subclavian_artery" title="Subclavian artery">left subclavian artery</a>, a branch off the aortic arch, sits in a groove from the arch to near the apex of the lung. A shallower groove in front of the artery and near the edge of the lung, lodges the left <a href="/wiki/Brachiocephalic_vein" title="Brachiocephalic vein">brachiocephalic vein</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> may sit in a wider shallow impression at the base of the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The weight of the left lung, by standard <a href="/wiki/Reference_range" title="Reference range">reference range</a>, in men is 110–675 g (0.243–1.488 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaDiMaio2012_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaDiMaio2012-11">[11]</a></sup> in women 105–515 g (0.231–1.135 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolinaDiMaio2015_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-MolinaDiMaio2015-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Microanatomy">Microanatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Microanatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lung_structure_normal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="372" data-file-width="475" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Lung_structure_normal.jpg/280px-Lung_structure_normal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Lung_structure_normal.jpg/420px-Lung_structure_normal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Lung_structure_normal.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lung_structure_normal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-sectional detail of the lung</div></div></div>
<p>The lungs are part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_respiratory_tract" title="Lower respiratory tract">lower respiratory tract</a>, and accommodate the bronchial airways when they branch from the trachea. The bronchial airways terminate in <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a> which make up the functional tissue (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lung_parenchyma" title="Lung parenchyma">parenchyma</a>) of the lung, and veins, arteries, nerves, and <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_vessel" title="Lymphatic vessel">lymphatic vessels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The trachea and bronchi have plexuses of <a href="/wiki/Lymph_capillary" title="Lymph capillary">lymph capillaries</a> in their mucosa and submucosa. The smaller bronchi have a single layer of lymph capillaries, and they are absent in the alveoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodora_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theodora-16">[16]</a></sup> The lungs are supplied with the largest lymphatic drainage system of any other organ in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saladin_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saladin-17">[17]</a></sup> Each lung is surrounded by a <a href="/wiki/Serous_membrane" title="Serous membrane">serous membrane</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_pleurae" title="Pulmonary pleurae">visceral pleura</a>, which has an underlying layer of <a href="/wiki/Loose_connective_tissue" title="Loose connective tissue">loose connective tissue</a> attached to the substance of the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorlands1077_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorlands1077-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connective_tissue">Connective tissue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Connective tissue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2456" data-file-width="2456" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png/220px-STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png/330px-STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png/440px-STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STD_190219_SWITCH_Tissue_2_93x_CMLE_20SNR_50IT_Elastin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thick <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_fibres" title="Elastic fibres">elastic fibres</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visceral_pleura" title="Visceral pleura">visceral pleura</a> (outer lining) of lung</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg/220px-Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg/330px-Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg/440px-Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fibers_of_Collagen_Type_I_-_TEM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">TEM</a> image of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collagen_fibres" title="Collagen fibres">collagen fibres</a> in a cross sectional slice of mammalian lung tissue.</div></div></div>
<p>The connective tissue of the lungs is made up of <a href="/wiki/Elastic_fiber" title="Elastic fiber">elastic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collagen_fibres" title="Collagen fibres">collagen fibres </a> that are interspersed between the capillaries and the alveolar walls. <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">Elastin</a> is the key <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">extracellular matrix</a> and is the main component of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_fibres" title="Elastic fibres">elastic fibres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mithieux_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mithieux-19">[19]</a></sup> Elastin gives the necessary elasticity and resilience required for the persistent stretching involved in breathing, known as <a href="/wiki/Lung_compliance" title="Lung compliance">lung compliance</a>. It is also responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Elastic_recoil" title="Elastic recoil">elastic recoil</a> needed. Elastin is more concentrated in areas of high stress such as the openings of the alveoli, and alveolar junctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mithieux_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mithieux-19">[19]</a></sup> The connective tissue links all the alveoli to form the lung parenchyma which has a sponge-like appearance. The alveoli have interconnecting air passages in their walls known as the <a href="/wiki/Pores_of_Kohn" title="Pores of Kohn">pores of Kohn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocock_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_epithelium">Respiratory epithelium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Respiratory epithelium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_epithelium" title="Respiratory epithelium">Respiratory epithelium</a></div>
<p>All of the lower respiratory tract including the trachea, bronchi, and bronchioles is lined with <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_epithelium" title="Respiratory epithelium">respiratory epithelium</a>. This is a <a href="/wiki/Cilium" title="Cilium">ciliated</a> epithelium interspersed with <a href="/wiki/Goblet_cell" title="Goblet cell">goblet cells</a> which produce <a href="/wiki/Mucin" title="Mucin">mucin</a> the main component of <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a>, ciliated cells, <a href="/wiki/Airway_basal_cell" title="Airway basal cell">basal cells</a>, and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_bronchiole" title="Terminal bronchiole">terminal bronchioles</a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Club_cells" title="Club cells">club cells</a> with actions similar to basal cells, and <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>. The epithelial cells, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submucosal_gland" title="Submucosal gland">submucosal glands</a> throughout the respiratory tract secrete <a href="/wiki/Mucus#Respiratory_system" title="Mucus">airway surface liquid</a> (ASL), the composition of which is tightly regulated and determines how well <a href="/wiki/Mucociliary_clearance" title="Mucociliary clearance">mucociliary clearance</a> works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Neuroendocrine_cell#Pulmonary_neuroendocrine_cells" title="Neuroendocrine cell">Pulmonary neuroendocrine cells</a> are found throughout the respiratory epithelium including the alveolar epithelium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Lommel_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Lommel-22">[22]</a></sup> though they only account for around 0.5 per cent of the total epithelial population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garg_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garg-23">[23]</a></sup> PNECs are innervated airway epithelial cells that are particularly focused at airway junction points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garg_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garg-23">[23]</a></sup> These cells can produce serotonin, dopamine, and norepinephrine, as well as polypeptide products. Cytoplasmic processes from the pulmonary neuroendocrine cells extend into the airway lumen where they may sense the composition of inspired gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberger_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberger-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronchial_airways">Bronchial airways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bronchial airways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the bronchi there are incomplete <a href="/wiki/Trachea#Structure" title="Trachea">tracheal rings</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a> and smaller plates of cartilage that keep them open.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuytonHall_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuytonHall-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 472">: 472 </span></sup> Bronchioles are too narrow to support cartilage and their walls are of <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a>, and this is largely absent in the narrower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_bronchiole" title="Respiratory bronchiole">respiratory bronchioles</a> which are mainly just of epithelium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuytonHall_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-GuytonHall-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 472">: 472 </span></sup> The absence of cartilage in the terminal bronchioles gives them an alternative name of <i>membranous bronchioles</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg/220px-Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg/330px-Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg/440px-Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Secondary-pulmonary-lobule-illustration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A lobule of the lung enclosed in septa and supplied by a terminal bronchiole that branches into the respiratory bronchioles. Each respiratory bronchiole supplies the alveoli held in each acinus accompanied by a pulmonary artery branch.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_zone">Respiratory zone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Respiratory zone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The conducting zone of the respiratory tract ends at the terminal bronchioles when they branch into the respiratory bronchioles. This marks the beginning of the terminal respiratory unit called the <b>acinus</b> which includes the respiratory bronchioles, the alveolar ducts, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_sac" title="Alveolar sac">alveolar sacs</a>, and alveoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberger2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberger2-27">[27]</a></sup> An acinus measures up to 10 mm in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hochhegger_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hochhegger-28">[28]</a></sup> A <b>primary pulmonary lobule</b> is that part of the acinus that includes the alveolar ducts, sacs, and alveoli but does not include the respiratory bronchioles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goel_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goel-29">[29]</a></sup> The unit described as the <b>secondary pulmonary lobule</b> is the lobule most referred to as the <b>pulmonary lobule</b> or <b>respiratory lobule</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuytonHall_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-GuytonHall-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 489">: 489 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaillard_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaillard-30">[30]</a></sup> This lobule is a discrete unit that is the smallest component of the lung that can be seen without aid. The secondary pulmonary lobule is likely to be made up of between 30 and 50 primary lobules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goel_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goel-29">[29]</a></sup> The lobule is supplied by a terminal bronchiole that branches into respiratory bronchioles. The respiratory bronchioles supply the alveoli in each acinus and is accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> branch. Each lobule is enclosed by an interlobular septa. Each acinus is incompletely separated by an interlobular septa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hochhegger_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hochhegger-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The respiratory bronchiole gives rise to the alveolar ducts that lead to the alveolar sacs, which contain two or more alveoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocock_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock-20">[20]</a></sup> The walls of the alveoli are extremely thin allowing a fast rate of <a href="/wiki/Diffusion#Diffusion_vs._bulk_flow" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a>.  The alveoli have interconnecting small air passages in their walls known as the <a href="/wiki/Pores_of_Kohn" title="Pores of Kohn">pores of Kohn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocock_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alveoli">Alveoli</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Alveoli">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">Pulmonary alveolus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alveolus_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="465" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Alveolus_diagram.svg/220px-Alveolus_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Alveolus_diagram.svg/330px-Alveolus_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Alveolus_diagram.svg/440px-Alveolus_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alveolus_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alveoli and their capillary networks.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg"><img alt="A 3D Medical illustration showing different terminating ends of Bronchial airways connected to alveoili, lung parenchyma &amp; lymphatic vessels." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg/220px-3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg/330px-3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg/440px-3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_Medical_Animation_Bronchial_Airways_terminating_ends.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>3D Medical illustration showing different terminating ends of bronchioles.</div></div></div>
<p>Alveoli consist of two types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_cell" title="Alveolar cell">alveolar cell</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_macrophage" title="Alveolar macrophage">alveolar macrophage</a>. The two types of cell are known as <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus#Type_I_cells" title="Pulmonary alveolus">type I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus#Type_II_cells" title="Pulmonary alveolus">type II cells</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup> (also known as pneumocytes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRAYS40TH_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-GRAYS40TH-3">[3]</a></sup> Types I and II make up the walls and <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_septum" title="Alveolar septum">alveolar septa</a>. Type I cells provide 95% of the surface area of each alveoli and are flat ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squamous_epithelial_cell" title="Squamous epithelial cell">squamous</a>"), and Type II cells generally cluster in the corners of the alveoli and have a cuboidal shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup> Despite this, cells occur in a roughly equal ratio of 1:1 or 6:4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type I are <a href="/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">squamous epithelial cells</a> that make up the alveolar wall structure. They have extremely thin walls that enable an easy gas exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup> These type I cells also make up the alveolar septa which separate each alveolus. The septa consist of an epithelial lining and associated <a href="/wiki/Basement_membrane" title="Basement membrane">basement membranes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup> Type I cells are not able to divide, and consequently rely on <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiation</a> from Type II cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type II are larger and they line the alveoli and produce and secrete epithelial lining fluid, and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_surfactant" title="Pulmonary surfactant">lung surfactant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26475269_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26475269-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup> Type II cells are able to divide and differentiate to Type I cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_macrophage" title="Alveolar macrophage">alveolar macrophages</a> have an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>. They remove substances which deposit in the alveoli including loose red blood cells that have been forced out from blood vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microbiota">Microbiota</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Microbiota">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lung_microbiota" title="Lung microbiota">Lung microbiota</a></div>
<p>There is a large presence of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> in the lungs known as the <a href="/wiki/Lung_microbiota" title="Lung microbiota">lung microbiota</a> that interacts with the airway epithelial cells; an interaction of probable importance in maintaining homeostasis. The <a href="/wiki/Microbiota#Humans" title="Microbiota">microbiota</a> is complex and dynamic in healthy people, and altered in diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COPD" title="COPD">COPD</a>. For example significant changes can take place in COPD following infection with <a href="/wiki/Rhinovirus" title="Rhinovirus">rhinovirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hiemstra_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hiemstra-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">Fungal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a> that are commonly found as <a href="/wiki/Mycobiota" title="Mycobiota">mycobiota</a> in the microbiota include <i><a href="/wiki/Candida_(fungus)" title="Candida (fungus)">Candida</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Malassezia" title="Malassezia">Malassezia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces" title="Saccharomyces">Saccharomyces</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus" title="Aspergillus">Aspergillus</a></i>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cui_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cui-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_tract">Respiratory tract</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Respiratory tract">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">Respiratory tract</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illu_conducting_passages.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="255" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Illu_conducting_passages.svg/170px-Illu_conducting_passages.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Illu_conducting_passages.svg/255px-Illu_conducting_passages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Illu_conducting_passages.svg/340px-Illu_conducting_passages.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illu_conducting_passages.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The lungs as main part of respiratory tract</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract#Lower_respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">lower respiratory tract</a> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a>, and consists of the <a href="/wiki/Trachea" title="Trachea">trachea</a> and the structures below this including the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup> The trachea receives air from the <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharynx</a> and travels down to a place where it splits (the <a href="/wiki/Carina_of_trachea" title="Carina of trachea">carina</a>) into a right and left primary <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchus</a>. These supply air to the right and left lungs, splitting progressively into the secondary and tertiary bronchi for the lobes of the lungs, and into smaller and smaller bronchioles until they become the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_bronchiole" title="Respiratory bronchiole">respiratory bronchioles</a>. These in turn supply air through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_duct" title="Alveolar duct">alveolar ducts</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">exchange of gases</a> take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup> Oxygen <a href="/wiki/Inhalation" title="Inhalation">breathed in</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_diffusion#Biology" title="Molecular diffusion">diffuses</a> through the walls of the alveoli into the enveloping <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a> and into the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocock_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock-20">[20]</a></sup> and carbon dioxide diffuses from the blood into the lungs to be <a href="/wiki/Exhalation" title="Exhalation">breathed out</a>.
</p><p>Estimates of the total surface area of lungs vary from 50 to 75 square metres (540 to 810 sq ft);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup> although this is often quoted in textbooks and the media being "the size of a tennis court",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> it is actually less than half the size of a <a href="/wiki/Tennis_court" title="Tennis court">singles court</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bronchi in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conducting_zone" title="Conducting zone">conducting zone</a> are reinforced with <a href="/wiki/Hyaline_cartilage" title="Hyaline cartilage">hyaline cartilage</a> in order to hold open the airways. The bronchioles have no cartilage and are surrounded instead by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle_tissue" title="Smooth muscle tissue">smooth muscle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup>  Air is warmed to 37 °C (99 °F), <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidified</a> and cleansed by the conducting zone. <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">Particles</a> from the air being removed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_epithelium" title="Respiratory epithelium">respiratory epithelium</a> lining the passageways,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmadi2013_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmadi2013-40">[40]</a></sup> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Mucociliary_clearance" title="Mucociliary clearance">mucociliary clearance</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_stretch_receptors" title="Pulmonary stretch receptors">Pulmonary stretch receptors</a> in the smooth muscle of the airways initiate a <a href="/wiki/Reflex" title="Reflex">reflex</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Hering%E2%80%93Breuer_reflex" title="Hering–Breuer reflex">Hering–Breuer reflex</a> that prevents the lungs from over-inflation, during forceful inspiration.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_supply">Blood supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Blood supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">Pulmonary circulation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_CT_of_thorax,_annotated.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1131" data-file-width="1544" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/3D_CT_of_thorax%2C_annotated.jpg/330px-3D_CT_of_thorax%2C_annotated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/3D_CT_of_thorax%2C_annotated.jpg/495px-3D_CT_of_thorax%2C_annotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/3D_CT_of_thorax%2C_annotated.jpg/660px-3D_CT_of_thorax%2C_annotated.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_CT_of_thorax,_annotated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/3D_rendering" title="3D rendering">3D rendering</a> of a <a href="/wiki/High-resolution_computed_tomography" title="High-resolution computed tomography">high-resolution CT scan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">thorax</a>. The anterior thoracic wall, the airways and the pulmonary vessels anterior to the <a href="/wiki/Root_of_the_lung" title="Root of the lung">root of the lung</a> have been digitally removed in order to visualize the different levels of the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The lungs have a dual blood supply provided by a <a href="/wiki/Bronchial_circulation" title="Bronchial circulation">bronchial</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_K_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_K-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bronchial_circulation" title="Bronchial circulation">bronchial circulation</a> supplies oxygenated blood to the airways of the lungs, through the <a href="/wiki/Bronchial_artery" title="Bronchial artery">bronchial arteries</a> that leave the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a>.  There are usually three arteries, two to the left lung and one to the right, and they branch alongside the bronchi and bronchioles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-7"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a> carries deoxygenated blood from the heart to the lungs and returns the oxygenated blood to the heart to supply the rest of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BERNELEVY_31-8"><a href="#cite_note-BERNELEVY-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The blood volume of the lungs is about 450 millilitres on average, about 9% of the total blood volume of the entire circulatory system. This quantity can easily fluctuate from between one-half and twice the normal volume.  Also, in the event of blood loss through hemorrhage, blood from the lungs can partially compensate by automatically transferring to the systemic circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nerve_supply">Nerve supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nerve supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The lungs are supplied by nerves of the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a>. Input from the <a href="/wiki/Parasympathetic_nervous_system" title="Parasympathetic nervous system">parasympathetic nervous system</a> occurs via the <a href="/wiki/Vagus_nerve" title="Vagus nerve">vagus nerve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_K_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_K-4">[4]</a></sup> When stimulated by <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a>, this causes constriction of the smooth muscle lining the bronchus and bronchioles, and increases the secretions from glands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levitzky2013_2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levitzky2013_2-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2018)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The lungs also have a sympathetic tone from <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> acting on the <a href="/wiki/Beta-2_adrenergic_receptor" title="Beta-2 adrenergic receptor">beta 2 adrenoceptors</a> in the respiratory tract, which causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronchodilation" title="Bronchodilation">bronchodilation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The action of breathing takes place because of nerve signals sent by the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_center" title="Respiratory center">respiratory center</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brainstem" title="Brainstem">brainstem</a>, along the <a href="/wiki/Phrenic_nerve" title="Phrenic nerve">phrenic nerve</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Cervical_plexus" title="Cervical plexus">cervical plexus</a> to the diaphragm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variation">Variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The lobes of the lung are subject to <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_variation" title="Anatomical variation">anatomical variations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore2-45">[45]</a></sup> A horizontal interlobar fissure was found to be incomplete in 25% of right lungs, or even absent in 11% of all cases. An accessory fissure was also found in 14% and 22% of left and right lungs, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> An oblique fissure was found to be incomplete in 21% to 47% of left lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In some cases a fissure is absent, or extra, resulting in a right lung with only two lobes, or a left lung with three lobes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore2-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>A variation in the airway branching structure has been found specifically in the central airway
branching. This variation is associated with the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COPD" title="COPD">COPD</a> in adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonic_hedgehog#Lung_development" title="Sonic hedgehog">Sonic hedgehog § Lung development</a></div>
<p>The development of the human lungs arise from the <a href="/wiki/Laryngotracheal_groove" title="Laryngotracheal groove">laryngotracheal groove</a> and develop to maturity over several weeks in the foetus and for several years following birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadler_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sadler-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trachea" title="Trachea">trachea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchi</a> and lungs that make up the respiratory tract, begin to form during the fourth week of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">embryogenesis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_2002_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_2002-50">[50]</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Lung_bud" title="Lung bud">lung bud</a> which appears ventrally to the caudal portion of the <a href="/wiki/Foregut" title="Foregut">foregut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray949.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Gray949.png/220px-Gray949.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Gray949.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray949.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lungs during development, showing the early branching of the primitive bronchial buds</div></div></div>
<p>The respiratory tract has a branching structure, and is also known as the respiratory tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miura_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miura-52">[52]</a></sup> In the embryo this structure is developed in the process of <a href="/wiki/Morphogenesis#Branching_morphogenesis" title="Morphogenesis">branching morphogenesis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ochoa-Espinosa_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ochoa-Espinosa-53">[53]</a></sup> and is generated by the repeated splitting of the tip of the branch. In the development of the lungs (as in some other organs) the epithelium forms branching tubes. The lung has a left-right symmetry and each bud known as a <a href="/wiki/Lung_bud" title="Lung bud">bronchial bud</a> grows out as a tubular epithelium that becomes a bronchus. Each bronchus branches into bronchioles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolpert_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert-54">[54]</a></sup> The branching is a result of the tip of each tube bifurcating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miura_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miura-52">[52]</a></sup> The branching process forms the bronchi, bronchioles, and ultimately the alveoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miura_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Miura-52">[52]</a></sup> The four genes mostly associated with branching morphogenesis in the lung are the <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog_signaling_pathway" title="Hedgehog signaling pathway">intercellular signalling protein</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonic_hedgehog" title="Sonic hedgehog">sonic hedgehog</a> (SHH), <a href="/wiki/Fibroblast_growth_factor" title="Fibroblast growth factor">fibroblast growth factors</a> <a href="/wiki/FGF10" title="FGF10">FGF10</a> and FGFR2b, and <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein" title="Bone morphogenetic protein">bone morphogenetic protein</a> <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein_4" title="Bone morphogenetic protein 4">BMP4</a>. FGF10 is seen to have the most prominent role. FGF10 is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracrine_signalling" title="Paracrine signalling">paracrine signalling</a> molecule needed for epithelial branching, and SHH inhibits FGF10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miura_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-Miura-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolpert_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpert-54">[54]</a></sup> The development of the alveoli is influenced by a different mechanism whereby continued bifurcation is stopped and the distal tips become dilated to form the alveoli.
</p><p>At the end of the fourth week the lung bud divides into two, the right and left <a href="/wiki/Lung_bud" title="Lung bud">primary bronchial buds</a> on each side of the trachea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsen2001_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larsen2001-56">[56]</a></sup> During the fifth week the right bud branches into three secondary bronchial buds and the left branches into two secondary bronchial buds. These give rise to the lobes of the lungs, three on the right and two on the left. Over the following week, the secondary buds branch into tertiary buds, about ten on each side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsen2001_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Larsen2001-56">[56]</a></sup> From the sixth week to the sixteenth week, the major elements of the lungs appear except the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-7817-5309-0_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-7817-5309-0-57">[57]</a></sup> From week 16 to week 26, the bronchi enlarge and lung tissue becomes highly vascularised. Bronchioles and alveolar ducts also develop. By week 26 the terminal bronchioles have formed which branch into two respiratory bronchioles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> During the period covering the 26th week until birth the important <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93air_barrier" title="Blood–air barrier">blood–air barrier</a> is established. Specialised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_cells" title="Alveolar cells">type I alveolar cells</a> where <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> will take place, together with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_cells" title="Alveolar cells">type II alveolar cells</a> that secrete <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_surfactant" title="Pulmonary surfactant">pulmonary surfactant</a>, appear. The surfactant reduces the <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a> at the air-alveolar surface which allows expansion of the alveolar sacs. The alveolar sacs contain the primitive alveoli that form at the end of the alveolar ducts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
and their appearance around the seventh month marks the point at which limited respiration would be possible, and the premature baby could survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadler_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sadler-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitamin_A_deficiency">Vitamin A deficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Vitamin A deficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A_deficiency" title="Vitamin A deficiency">Vitamin A deficiency</a></div>
<p>The developing lung is particularly vulnerable to changes in the levels of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A_deficiency" title="Vitamin A deficiency">Vitamin A deficiency</a> has been linked to changes in the epithelial lining of the lung and in the lung parenchyma. This can disrupt the normal physiology of the lung and predispose to respiratory diseases. Severe nutritional deficiency in vitamin A results in a reduction in the formation of the alveolar walls (septa) and to notable changes in the respiratory epithelium; alterations are noted in the extracellular matrix and in the protein content of the basement membrane. The extracellular matrix maintains lung elasticity; the basement membrane is associated with alveolar epithelium and is important in the blood-air barrier. The deficiency is associated with functional defects and disease states. Vitamin A is crucial in the development of the alveoli which continues for several years after birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timoneda_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Timoneda-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_birth">After birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: After birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">birth</a>, the baby's lungs are filled with fluid secreted by the lungs and are not inflated. <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_extrauterine_life#Breathing_and_circulation" title="Adaptation to extrauterine life">After birth</a> the infant's <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> reacts to the sudden change in temperature and environment. This triggers the first breath, within about 10 seconds after delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medline2015_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Medline2015-61">[61]</a></sup> Before birth, the lungs are filled with fetal lung fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>      After the first breath, the fluid is quickly absorbed into the body or exhaled. The <a href="/wiki/Vascular_resistance" title="Vascular resistance">resistance</a> in the lung's blood vessels decreases giving an increased surface area for gas exchange, and the lungs begin to breathe spontaneously. This accompanies <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_extrauterine_life" title="Adaptation to extrauterine life">other changes</a> which result in an increased amount of blood entering the lung tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medline2015_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Medline2015-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>At birth the lungs are very undeveloped with only around one sixth of the alveoli of the adult lung present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadler_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sadler-49">[49]</a></sup> The alveoli continue to form into early adulthood, and their ability to form when necessary is seen in the regeneration of the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schittny_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schittny-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchittnyJC_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchittnyJC-64">[64]</a></sup> Alveolar septa have a double <a href="/wiki/Capillary#Structure" title="Capillary">capillary network</a> instead of the single network of the developed lung. Only after the maturation of the capillary network can the lung enter a normal phase of growth. Following the early growth in numbers of alveoli there is another stage of the alveoli being enlarged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Function">Function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">Respiratory system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">Breathing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">Gas exchange</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_exchange">Gas exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Gas exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The major function of the lungs is <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> between the lungs and the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveolar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary capillary</a> gases equilibrate across the thin <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93air_barrier" title="Blood–air barrier">blood–air barrier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26475269_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26475269-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grays_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-grays-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s-cool_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-s-cool-68">[68]</a></sup> This thin membrane (about 0.5 –2 μm thick) is folded into about 300 million alveoli, providing an extremely large surface area (estimates varying between 70 and 145 m<sup>2</sup>) for gas exchange to occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grays_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-grays-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2380" data-file-width="3003" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg/220px-Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg/330px-Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg/440px-Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ribcage_during_inhalation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The effect of the <a href="/wiki/Muscles_of_respiration" title="Muscles of respiration">respiratory muscles</a> in expanding the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a>.</div></div></div>
