<p>The lungs are not capable of expanding to <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">breathe</a> on their own, and will only do so when there is an increase in the volume of the thoracic cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levitzky2013_1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levitzky2013_1-70">[70]</a></sup> This is achieved by the <a href="/wiki/Muscles_of_respiration" title="Muscles of respiration">muscles of respiration</a>, through the contraction of the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Intercostal_muscles" title="Intercostal muscles">intercostal muscles</a> which pull the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a> upwards as shown in the diagram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Exhalation" title="Exhalation">breathing out</a> the muscles relax, returning the lungs to their resting position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tortora1_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-tortora1-72">[72]</a></sup> At this point the lungs contain the <a href="/wiki/Functional_residual_capacity" title="Functional residual capacity">functional residual capacity</a> (FRC) of air, which, in the adult human, has a volume of about 2.5–3.0 litres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tortora1_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-tortora1-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperpnoea" title="Hyperpnoea">heavy breathing</a> as in <a href="/wiki/Exertion" title="Exertion">exertion</a>, a large number of <a href="/wiki/Muscles_of_respiration#Accessory_muscles" title="Muscles of respiration">accessory muscles</a> in the neck and abdomen are recruited, that during exhalation pull the ribcage down, decreasing the volume of the thoracic cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tortora1_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-tortora1-72">[72]</a></sup> The FRC is now decreased, but since the lungs cannot be emptied completely there is still about a litre of residual air left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tortora1_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-tortora1-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="#Function_testing">Lung function testing</a> is carried out to evaluate <a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">lung volumes</a> and capacities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection">Protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The lungs possess several characteristics which protect against infection. The respiratory tract is lined by <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_epithelium" title="Respiratory epithelium">respiratory epithelium</a> or respiratory mucosa, with hair-like projections called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> that beat rhythmically and carry <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Mucociliary_clearance" title="Mucociliary clearance">mucociliary clearance</a> is an important defence system against air-borne infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26475269_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26475269-33">[33]</a></sup> The dust particles and bacteria in the inhaled air are caught in the mucosal surface of the airways, and are moved up towards the pharynx by the rhythmic upward beating action of the cilia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 661–730">: 661–730 </span></sup> The lining of the lung also secretes <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_A" title="Immunoglobulin A">immunoglobulin A</a> which protects against respiratory infections;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goblet_cells" title="Goblet cells">goblet cells</a> secrete mucus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAWLINA_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-PAWLINA-32">[32]</a></sup> which also contains several antimicrobial compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Defensin" title="Defensin">defensins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiprotease" title="Antiprotease">antiproteases</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> A rare type of specialised cell called a <b>pulmonary ionocyte</b> that is suggested may regulate mucus viscosity has been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFN_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-CFN-76">[76]</a></sup> In addition, the lining of the lung also contains <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>, immune cells which engulf and destroy debris and microbes that enter the lung in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a> which present antigens to activate components of the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a> such as <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The size of the respiratory tract and the flow of air also protect the lungs from larger particles. Smaller particles deposit in the <a href="/wiki/Human_mouth" title="Human mouth">mouth</a> and behind the mouth in the <a href="/wiki/Pharynx#Oropharynx" title="Pharynx">oropharynx</a>, and larger particles are trapped in <a href="/wiki/Nasal_hair" title="Nasal hair">nasal hair</a> after inhalation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to their function in respiration, the lungs have a number of other functions. They are involved in maintaining <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>, helping in the regulation of <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Renin%E2%80%93angiotensin_system" title="Renin–angiotensin system">renin–angiotensin system</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">inner lining</a> of the blood vessels secretes <a href="/wiki/Angiotensin-converting_enzyme" title="Angiotensin-converting enzyme">angiotensin-converting enzyme</a> (ACE) an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyses</a> the conversion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiotensin_I" title="Angiotensin I">angiotensin I</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiotensin_II" title="Angiotensin II">angiotensin II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boron_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-boron-77">[77]</a></sup> The lungs are involved in the blood's <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_homeostasis" title="Acid–base homeostasis">acid–base homeostasis</a> by expelling <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> when breathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levitzky2013_1_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levitzky2013_1-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PatientInfo_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-PatientInfo-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lungs also serve a protective role. Several blood-borne substances, such as a few types of <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukotrienes" title="Leukotrienes">leukotrienes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bradykinin" title="Bradykinin">bradykinin</a>, are excreted through the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boron_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-boron-77">[77]</a></sup> Drugs and other substances can be absorbed, modified or excreted in the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levitzky2013_1_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levitzky2013_1-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smyth_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth-79">[79]</a></sup> The lungs filter out small <a href="/wiki/Thrombus" title="Thrombus">blood clots</a> from <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> and prevent them from entering <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a> and causing <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PatientInfo_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-PatientInfo-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lungs also play a pivotal role in <a href="/wiki/Speech" title="Speech">speech</a> by providing air and airflow for the creation of vocal sounds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levitzky2013_1_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Levitzky2013_1-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and other <a href="/wiki/Paralanguage" title="Paralanguage">paralanguage</a> <a href="/wiki/Paralanguage#Specific_forms_of_paralinguistic_respiration" title="Paralanguage">communications</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sighing" title="Sighing">sighs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasp" title="Gasp">gasps</a>.
</p><p>Research suggests a role of the lungs in the production of blood platelets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gene_and_protein_expression">Gene and protein expression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Gene and protein expression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics#Gene_and_protein_expression" title="Bioinformatics">Bioinformatics § Gene and protein expression</a></div>
<p>About 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Human_genome#Coding_sequences_(protein-coding_genes)" title="Human genome">protein coding genes</a> are expressed in human cells and almost 75% of these genes are expressed in the normal lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> A little less than 200 of these genes are more specifically expressed in the lung with less than 20 genes being highly lung specific. The highest expression of lung specific proteins are different <a href="/wiki/Surfactant" title="Surfactant">surfactant</a> proteins,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26475269_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26475269-33">[33]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Surfactant_protein_A1" title="Surfactant protein A1">SFTPA1</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surfactant_protein_B" title="Surfactant protein B">SFTPB</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surfactant_protein_C" title="Surfactant protein C">SFTPC</a>, and <a href="/wiki/NAPSA" title="NAPSA">napsin</a>, expressed in type II pneumocytes. Other proteins with elevated expression in the lung are the <a href="/wiki/Dynein" title="Dynein">dynein</a> protein <a href="/wiki/DNAH5" title="DNAH5">DNAH5</a> in ciliated cells, and the secreted <a href="/wiki/Uteroglobin" title="Uteroglobin">SCGB1A1</a> protein in mucus-secreting  <a href="/wiki/Goblet_cell" title="Goblet cell">goblet cells</a> of the airway mucosa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_significance">Clinical significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Clinical significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_disease" title="Respiratory disease">Respiratory disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonology" title="Pulmonology">Pulmonology</a></div>
<p>Lungs can be affected by a number of diseases and disorders. <a href="/wiki/Pulmonology" title="Pulmonology">Pulmonology</a> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specialty_(medicine)" title="Specialty (medicine)">medical speciality</a> that deals with <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_disease" title="Respiratory disease">respiratory diseases</a> involving the lungs and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACP_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACP-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cardiothoracic_surgery" title="Cardiothoracic surgery">Cardiothoracic surgery</a> deals with <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> of the lungs including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lung_volume_reduction_surgery" title="Lung volume reduction surgery">lung volume reduction surgery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobectomy" title="Lobectomy">lobectomy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pneumectomy" title="Pneumectomy">pneumectomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lung_transplantation" title="Lung transplantation">lung transplantation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflammation_and_infection">Inflammation and infection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Inflammation and infection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ghon_focus" title="Ghon focus">Ghon focus</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">Inflammatory</a> conditions of the lung tissue are <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, of the respiratory tract are <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronchiolitis" title="Bronchiolitis">bronchiolitis</a>, and of the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_pleurae" title="Pulmonary pleurae">pleurae</a> surrounding the lungs <a href="/wiki/Pleurisy" title="Pleurisy">pleurisy</a>. Inflammation is usually caused by <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> or <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>. When the lung tissue is inflamed due to other causes it is called <a href="/wiki/Pneumonitis" title="Pneumonitis">pneumonitis</a>. One major cause of <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_pneumonia" title="Bacterial pneumonia">bacterial pneumonia</a> is <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chronic_condition" title="Chronic condition">Chronic</a> infections often occur in those with <a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">immunodeficiency</a> and can include a <a href="/wiki/Fungal_infection" title="Fungal infection">fungal infection</a> by <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus_fumigatus" title="Aspergillus fumigatus">Aspergillus fumigatus</a></i> that can lead to an <a href="/wiki/Aspergilloma" title="Aspergilloma">aspergilloma</a> forming in the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-6"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alcohol affects the lungs and can cause inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_lung_disease" title="Alcoholic lung disease">alcoholic lung disease</a>. Acute exposure to alcohol stimulates the beating of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> in the respiratory epithelium. However, chronic exposure has the effect of desensitising the ciliary response which reduces <a href="/wiki/Mucociliary_clearance" title="Mucociliary clearance">mucociliary clearance</a> (MCC). MCC is an innate defense system protecting against pollutants and pathogens, and when this is disrupted the numbers of <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_macrophage" title="Alveolar macrophage">alveolar macrophages</a> are decreased. A subsequent inflammatory response is the release of <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>. Another consequence is the susceptibility to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arvers_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arvers-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slovinsky_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slovinsky-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood-supply_changes">Blood-supply changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Blood-supply changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="1094" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG/220px-InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG/330px-InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG/440px-InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InfarctPandLbasilarsegmentsPE.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Infarction" title="Infarction">Tissue death</a> of the lung due to a <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a> is a blood clot that becomes lodged in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_arteries" title="Pulmonary arteries">pulmonary arteries</a>. The majority of emboli arise because of <a href="/wiki/Deep_vein_thrombosis" title="Deep vein thrombosis">deep vein thrombosis</a> in the legs. Pulmonary emboli may be investigated using a <a href="/wiki/Ventilation/perfusion_scan" title="Ventilation/perfusion scan">ventilation/perfusion scan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CT_pulmonary_angiography" title="CT pulmonary angiography">a CT scan of the arteries of the lung</a>, or blood tests such as the <a href="/wiki/D-dimer" title="D-dimer">D-dimer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-7"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_hypertension" title="Pulmonary hypertension">Pulmonary hypertension</a> describes an increased pressure at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> that has a large number of differing causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-8"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> Other rarer conditions may also affect the blood supply of the lung, such as <a href="/wiki/Granulomatosis_with_polyangiitis" title="Granulomatosis with polyangiitis">granulomatosis with polyangiitis</a>, which causes inflammation of the small blood vessels of the lungs and kidneys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-9"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_contusion" title="Pulmonary contusion">lung contusion</a> is a bruise caused by chest trauma. It results in hemorrhage of the alveoli causing a build-up of fluid which can impair breathing, and this can be either mild or severe.
The function of the lungs can also be affected by compression from fluid in the pleural cavity <a href="/wiki/Pleural_effusion" title="Pleural effusion">pleural effusion</a>, or other substances such as air (<a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">pneumothorax</a>), blood (<a href="/wiki/Hemothorax" title="Hemothorax">hemothorax</a>), or rarer causes. These may be investigated using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_X-ray" title="Chest X-ray">chest X-ray</a> or <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a>, and may require the insertion of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_drain" title="Surgical drain">surgical drain</a> until the underlying cause is identified and treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-10"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obstructive_lung_diseases">Obstructive lung diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Obstructive lung diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Respiratory_tract.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Respiratory_tract.jpg/220px-Respiratory_tract.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Respiratory_tract.jpg/330px-Respiratory_tract.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Respiratory_tract.jpg/440px-Respiratory_tract.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Respiratory_tract.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>3D still image of constricted airways as in bronchial asthma.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="528" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg/220px-Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg/330px-Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg/440px-Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emphysema_H_and_E.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lung tissue affected by emphysema using <a href="/wiki/H%26E_stain" title="H&amp;E stain">H&amp;E stain</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">Asthma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchiectasis" title="Bronchiectasis">bronchiectasis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> (COPD) that includes <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis#Chronic_bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">chronic bronchitis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emphysema" title="Emphysema">emphysema</a>, are all <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_lung_disease" title="Obstructive lung disease">obstructive lung diseases</a> characterised by <a href="/wiki/Airway_obstruction" title="Airway obstruction">airway obstruction</a>. This limits the amount of air that is able to enter alveoli because of constriction of the bronchial tree, due to inflammation. Obstructive lung diseases are often identified because of symptoms and diagnosed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_function_tests" title="Pulmonary function tests">pulmonary function tests</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Spirometry" title="Spirometry">spirometry</a>. Many obstructive lung diseases are managed by avoiding triggers (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dust_mite" title="Dust mite">dust mites</a> or <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a>), with symptom control such as <a href="/wiki/Bronchodilator" title="Bronchodilator">bronchodilators</a>, and with suppression of inflammation (such as through <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">corticosteroids</a>) in severe cases. A common cause of chronic bronchitis, and emphysema, is smoking; and common causes of <a href="/wiki/Bronchiectasis" title="Bronchiectasis">bronchiectasis</a> include severe infections and <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>. The definitive cause of <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> is not yet known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-11"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The breakdown of alveolar tissue, often as a result of tobacco-smoking leads to emphysema, which can become severe enough to develop into COPD. <a href="/wiki/Elastase" title="Elastase">Elastase</a> breaks down the <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a> in the lung's connective tissue that can also result in emphysema. Elastase is inhibited by the <a href="/wiki/Acute-phase_protein" title="Acute-phase protein">acute-phase protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpha-1_antitrypsin" title="Alpha-1 antitrypsin">alpha-1 antitrypsin</a>, and when there is a <a href="/wiki/Alpha-1_antitrypsin_deficiency" title="Alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency">deficiency</a> in this, emphysema can develop. With persistent stress from smoking, the <a href="/wiki/Airway_basal_cell" title="Airway basal cell">airway basal cells</a> become disarranged and lose their regenerative ability needed to repair the epithelial barrier. The disorganised basal cells are seen to be responsible for the major airway changes that are characteristic of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COPD" title="COPD">COPD</a>, and with continued stress can undergo a malignant transformation. Studies have shown that the initial development of emphysema is centred on the early changes in the airway epithelium of the small airways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crystal_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crystal-90">[90]</a></sup> Basal cells become further deranged in a smoker's transition to clinically defined COPD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crystal_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crystal-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restrictive_lung_diseases">Restrictive lung diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Restrictive lung diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some types of chronic lung diseases are classified as <a href="/wiki/Restrictive_lung_disease" title="Restrictive lung disease">restrictive lung disease</a>, because of a restriction in the amount of lung tissue involved in respiration. These include <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_fibrosis" title="Pulmonary fibrosis">pulmonary fibrosis</a> which can occur when the lung is inflamed for a long period of time. <a href="/wiki/Fibrosis" title="Fibrosis">Fibrosis</a> in the lung replaces functioning lung tissue with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibrous_connective_tissue" title="Fibrous connective tissue">fibrous connective tissue</a>. This can be due to a large variety of <a href="/wiki/Occupational_lung_disease" title="Occupational lung disease">occupational lung diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coalworker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coalworker's pneumoconiosis">Coalworker's pneumoconiosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_disease" title="Autoimmune disease">autoimmune diseases</a> or more rarely to a reaction to <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-12"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> Severe respiratory disorders, where spontaneous breathing is not enough to maintain life, may need the use of <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilation" title="Mechanical ventilation">mechanical ventilation</a> to ensure an adequate supply of air.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancers">Cancers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cancers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">Lung cancer</a> can either arise directly from lung tissue or as a result of <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a> from another part of the body. There are two main types of primary tumour described as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small-cell_lung_carcinoma" title="Small-cell lung carcinoma">small-cell</a> or <a href="/wiki/Non-small-cell_lung_carcinoma" title="Non-small-cell lung carcinoma">non-small-cell lung carcinomas</a>. The major risk factor for cancer is <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>. Once a cancer is identified it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumour_staging" title="Tumour staging">staged</a> using scans such as a <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a> and a sample of tissue from a <a href="/wiki/Biopsy" title="Biopsy">biopsy</a> is taken. Cancers may be treated surgically by removing the tumour, the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> or a combination, or with the aim of <a href="/wiki/Palliative_care" title="Palliative care">symptom control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-13"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer_screening" title="Lung cancer screening">Lung cancer screening</a> is being recommended in the United States for high-risk populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congenital_disorders">Congenital disorders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Congenital disorders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_disorder" title="Congenital disorder">Congenital disorders</a> include <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_hypoplasia" title="Pulmonary hypoplasia">pulmonary hypoplasia</a> (an incomplete development of the lungs)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cadichon_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cadichon-92">[92]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Congenital_diaphragmatic_hernia" title="Congenital diaphragmatic hernia">congenital diaphragmatic hernia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infant_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Infant respiratory distress syndrome">infant respiratory distress syndrome</a> caused by a deficiency in lung surfactant. An <a href="/wiki/Azygos_lobe" title="Azygos lobe">azygos lobe</a> is a congenital <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_variation" title="Anatomical variation">anatomical variation</a> which though usually without effect can cause problems in <a href="/wiki/Thoracoscopy" title="Thoracoscopy">thoracoscopic</a> procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pleural_space_pressure">Pleural space pressure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Pleural space pressure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">pneumothorax</a> (collapsed lung) is an abnormal collection of air in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleural_space" title="Pleural space">pleural space</a> that causes an uncoupling of the lung from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_wall" title="Chest wall">chest wall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2014_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2014-94">[94]</a></sup> The lung cannot expand against the air pressure inside the pleural space. An easy to understand example is a traumatic pneumothorax, where air enters the pleural space from outside the body, as occurs with puncture to the chest wall. Similarly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scuba_diver" title="Scuba diver">scuba divers</a> ascending while holding their breath with their lungs fully inflated can cause air sacs (<a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a>) to burst and leak high pressure air into the pleural space.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examination">Examination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Examination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_examination" title="Respiratory examination">Respiratory examination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_sounds" title="Respiratory sounds">Respiratory sounds</a></div>
<p>As part of a <a href="/wiki/Physical_examination" title="Physical examination">physical examination</a> in response to respiratory symptoms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyspnea" title="Dyspnea">shortness of breath</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">cough</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_examination" title="Respiratory examination">lung examination</a> may be carried out. This exam includes <a href="/wiki/Palpation" title="Palpation">palpation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">auscultation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The areas of the lungs that can be <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">listened to</a> using a stethoscope are called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lung_fields" title="Lung fields">lung fields</a>, and these are the posterior, lateral, and anterior lung fields. The posterior fields can be listened to from the back and include: the lower lobes (taking up three quarters of the posterior fields); the anterior fields taking up the other quarter; and the lateral fields under the <a href="/wiki/Axilla" title="Axilla">axillae</a>, the left axilla for the lingual, the right axilla for the middle right lobe. The anterior fields can also be auscultated from the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> An area known as the <a href="/wiki/Triangle_of_auscultation" title="Triangle of auscultation">triangle of auscultation</a> is an area of thinner musculature on the back which allows improved listening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malik_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malik-97">[97]</a></sup> Abnormal <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_sounds" title="Respiratory sounds">breathing sounds</a> heard during a lung exam can indicate the presence of a lung condition; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheezing" title="Wheezing">wheezing</a> for example is commonly associated with <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COPD" title="COPD">COPD</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Function_testing">Function testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Function testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_function_testing" title="Pulmonary function testing">Pulmonary function testing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">Lung volumes</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:592px;max-width:592px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:397px;max-width:397px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:136px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LungVolume.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="323" data-file-width="935" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/LungVolume.jpg/395px-LungVolume.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/LungVolume.jpg/593px-LungVolume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/LungVolume.jpg/790px-LungVolume.jpg 2x" width="395"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">Lung volumes</a> as described in the text.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:191px;max-width:191px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:136px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DoingSpirometry.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1092" data-file-width="1508" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DoingSpirometry.JPG/189px-DoingSpirometry.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DoingSpirometry.JPG/284px-DoingSpirometry.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DoingSpirometry.JPG/378px-DoingSpirometry.JPG 2x" width="189"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A person doing a <a href="/wiki/Spirometry" title="Spirometry">spirometry</a> test.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_function_testing" title="Pulmonary function testing">Lung function testing</a> is carried out by evaluating a person's capacity to inhale and exhale in different circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-98">[98]</a></sup> The volume of air inhaled and exhaled by a person at rest is the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_volume" title="Tidal volume">tidal volume</a> (normally 500-750mL); the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inspiratory_reserve_volume" title="Inspiratory reserve volume">inspiratory reserve volume</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expiratory_reserve_volume" title="Expiratory reserve volume">expiratory reserve volume</a> are the additional amounts a person is able to forcibly inhale and exhale respectively. The summed total of forced inspiration and expiration is a person's <a href="/wiki/Vital_capacity" title="Vital capacity">vital capacity</a>. Not all air is expelled from the lungs even after a forced breath out; the remainder of the air is called the <a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">residual volume</a>. Together these terms are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Lung_volumes" title="Lung volumes">lung volumes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pulmonary <a href="/wiki/Plethysmograph" title="Plethysmograph">plethysmographs</a> are used to measure <a href="/wiki/Functional_residual_capacity" title="Functional residual capacity">functional residual capacity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Functional residual capacity cannot be measured by tests that rely on breathing out, as a person is only able to breathe a maximum of 80% of their total functional capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applegate2014_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applegate2014-100">[100]</a></sup> The total lung capacity depends on the person's age, height, weight, and sex, and normally ranges between 4 and 6 litres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-98">[98]</a></sup> Females tend to have a 20–25% lower capacity than males. Tall people tend to have a larger total lung capacity than shorter people. <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">Smokers</a> have a lower capacity than nonsmokers. Thinner persons tend to have a larger capacity. Lung capacity can be increased by physical training as much as 40% but the effect may be modified by exposure to air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applegate2014_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Applegate2014-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laeremans_et_al._2018_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laeremans_et_al._2018-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other lung function tests include <a href="/wiki/Spirometry" title="Spirometry">spirometry</a>, measuring the amount (volume) and flow of air that can be inhaled and exhaled. The maximum volume of breath that can be exhaled is called the <a href="/wiki/Vital_capacity" title="Vital capacity">vital capacity</a>. In particular, how much a person is able to exhale in one second (called <a href="/wiki/Spirometry#Parameters" title="Spirometry">forced expiratory volume</a> (FEV1)) as a proportion of how much they are able to exhale in total (FEV). This ratio, the FEV1/FEV ratio, is important to distinguish whether a lung disease is <a href="/wiki/Restrictive_lung_disease" title="Restrictive lung disease">restrictive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Obstructive_lung_disease" title="Obstructive lung disease">obstructive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAVIDSONS2014_73-14"><a href="#cite_note-DAVIDSONS2014-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong2012_34_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong2012_34-98">[98]</a></sup> Another test is that of the lung's  <a href="/wiki/Diffusing_capacity" title="Diffusing capacity">diffusing capacity</a> – this is a measure of the transfer of gas from air to the blood in the lung capillaries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy#Respiratory_system" title="Bird anatomy">Bird anatomy § Respiratory system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BirdRespiration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3395" data-file-width="2803" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/BirdRespiration.svg/220px-BirdRespiration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/BirdRespiration.svg/330px-BirdRespiration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/BirdRespiration.svg/440px-BirdRespiration.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BirdRespiration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>On inhalation, air travels to air sacs near the back of a bird. The air then passes through the lungs to air sacs near the front of the bird, from where the air is exhaled.</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross-current_exchanger.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Cross-current_exchanger.jpg/220px-Cross-current_exchanger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Cross-current_exchanger.jpg/330px-Cross-current_exchanger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Cross-current_exchanger.jpg/440px-Cross-current_exchanger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross-current_exchanger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cross-current respiratory gas exchanger in the lungs of birds. Air is forced from the air sacs unidirectionally (from left to right in the diagram) through the parabronchi. The pulmonary capillaries surround the parabronchi in the manner shown (blood flowing from below the parabronchus to above it in the diagram).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvResp_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-AvResp-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-graham-103">[103]</a></sup> Blood or air with a high oxygen content is shown in red; oxygen-poor air or blood is shown in various shades of purple-blue.</div></div></div>
<p>The lungs of birds are relatively small, but are connected to 8 or 9 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_sacs" title="Air sacs">air sacs</a> that extend through much of the body, and are in turn connected to air spaces within the bones. On inhalation, air travels through the trachea of a bird into the air sacs. Air then travels continuously from the air sacs at the back, through the lungs, which are relatively fixed in size, to the air sacs at the front. From here, the air is exhaled. These fixed size lungs are called "circulatory lungs", as distinct from the "bellows-type lungs" found in most other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvResp_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-AvResp-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maina2005_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maina2005-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lungs of birds contain millions of tiny parallel passages called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parabronchi" title="Parabronchi">parabronchi</a>. Small sacs called <i>atria</i> radiate from the walls of the tiny passages; these, like the alveoli in other lungs, are the site of <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> by simple diffusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maina2005_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maina2005-104">[104]</a></sup> The blood flow around the parabronchi and their atria forms a cross-current process of gas exchange (see diagram on the right).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvResp_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-AvResp-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-graham_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-graham-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The air sacs, which hold air, do not contribute much to gas exchange, despite being thin-walled, as they are poorly vascularised. The air sacs expand and contract due to changes in the volume in the thorax and abdomen. This volume change is caused by the movement of the sternum and ribs and this movement is often synchronised with movement of the flight muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-VB-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Parabronchi in which the air flow is unidirectional are called <i>paleopulmonic parabronchi</i> and are found in all birds. Some birds, however, have, in addition, a lung structure where the air flow in the parabronchi is bidirectional. These are termed <i>neopulmonic parabronchi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maina2005_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-Maina2005-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptiles">Reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reptile_anatomy#Respiratory_system" title="Reptile anatomy">Reptile anatomy § Respiratory system</a></div>
<p>The lungs of most reptiles have a single bronchus running down the centre, from which numerous branches reach out to individual pockets throughout the lungs. These pockets are similar to alveoli in mammals, but much larger and fewer in number. These give the lung a sponge-like texture. In <a href="/wiki/Tuatara" title="Tuatara">tuataras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, the lungs are simpler in structure, similar to that of typical amphibians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-VB-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Snakes and limbless lizards typically possess only the right lung as a major respiratory organ; the left lung is greatly reduced, or even absent. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphisbaenian" title="Amphisbaenian">Amphisbaenians</a>, however, have the opposite arrangement, with a major left lung, and a reduced or absent right lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-VB-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodylia" title="Crocodylia">crocodilians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monitor_Lizard" title="Monitor Lizard">monitor lizards</a> have lungs similar to those of birds, providing a unidirectional airflow and even possessing air sacs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The now extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pterosauria" title="Pterosauria">pterosaurs</a> have seemingly even further refined this type of lung, extending the airsacs into the wing membranes and, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Lonchodectidae" title="Lonchodectidae">lonchodectids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tupuxuara" title="Tupuxuara">tupuxuara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Azhdarchoidea" title="Azhdarchoidea">azhdarchoids</a>, the hindlimbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">Reptilian</a> lungs typically receive air via expansion and contraction of the ribs driven by <a href="/wiki/Axial_skeleton" title="Axial skeleton">axial muscles</a> and buccal pumping. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodilian" title="Crocodilian">Crocodilians</a> also rely on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hepatic" title="Hepatic">hepatic</a> piston method, in which the liver is pulled back by a muscle anchored to the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubic bone</a> (part of the pelvis) called the diaphragmaticus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munns_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munns-108">[108]</a></sup> which in turn creates negative pressure in the crocodile's thoracic cavity, allowing air to be moved into the lungs by Boyle's law. <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">Turtles</a>, which are unable to move their ribs, instead use their forelimbs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pectoral_girdle" title="Pectoral girdle">pectoral girdle</a> to force air in and out of the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-VB-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphibians">Amphibians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Amphibians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Frog#Respiration_and_circulation" title="Frog">Frog § Respiration and circulation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Axolotl_ganz.jpg"><img alt="Axolotl" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="2178" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Axolotl_ganz.jpg/220px-Axolotl_ganz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Axolotl_ganz.jpg/330px-Axolotl_ganz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Axolotl_ganz.jpg/440px-Axolotl_ganz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Axolotl_ganz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Axolotl" title="Axolotl">axolotl</a> (<i>Ambystoma mexicanum</i>) retains its larval form with gills into adulthood</div></div></div>
<p>The lungs of most <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> are simple and balloon-like, with gas exchange limited to the outer surface of the lung. This is not very efficient, but amphibians have low metabolic demands and can also quickly dispose of carbon dioxide by diffusion across their skin in water, and supplement their oxygen supply by the same method. Amphibians employ a <a href="/wiki/Positive_pressure" title="Positive pressure">positive pressure</a> system to get air to their lungs, forcing air down into the lungs by <a href="/wiki/Buccal_pumping" title="Buccal pumping">buccal pumping</a>. This is distinct from most higher vertebrates, who use a breathing system driven by <a href="/wiki/Negative_room_pressure" title="Negative room pressure">negative pressure</a> where the lungs are inflated by expanding the rib cage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breathing_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breathing-109">[109]</a></sup> In buccal pumping, the floor of the mouth is lowered, filling the mouth cavity with air. The throat muscles then presses the throat against the underside of the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a>, forcing the air into the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-review-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the possibility of respiration across the skin combined with small size, all known lungless <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> are amphibians. The majority of salamander species are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungless_salamander" title="Lungless salamander">lungless salamanders</a>, which respirate through their skin and tissues lining their mouth. This necessarily restricts their size: all are small and rather thread-like in appearance, maximising skin surface relative to body volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duellman&amp;Trueb_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duellman&amp;Trueb-111">[111]</a></sup> Other known lungless tetrapods are the <a href="/wiki/Bornean_flat-headed_frog" title="Bornean flat-headed frog">Bornean flat-headed frog</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atretochoana_eiselti" title="Atretochoana eiselti">Atretochoana eiselti</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Caecilian" title="Caecilian">caecilian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lungs of amphibians typically have a few narrow internal walls (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/septum" title="wikt:septum">septa</a>) of soft tissue around the outer walls, increasing the respiratory surface area and giving the lung a honeycomb appearance. In some salamanders even these are lacking, and the lung has a smooth wall. In caecilians, as in snakes, only the right lung attains any size or development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_105-4"><a href="#cite_note-VB-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lungfish">Lungfish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Lungfish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The lungs of <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a> are similar to those of amphibians, with few, if any, internal septa. In the <a href="/wiki/Australian_lungfish" title="Australian lungfish">Australian lungfish</a>, there is only a single lung, albeit divided into two lobes. Other lungfish and <i><a href="/wiki/Polypterus" title="Polypterus">Polypterus</a></i>, however, have two lungs, which are located in the upper part of the body, with the connecting duct curving around and above the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>. The blood supply also twists around the esophagus, suggesting that the lungs originally evolved in the ventral part of the body, as in other vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_105-5"><a href="#cite_note-VB-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Invertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system_of_gastropods" title="Respiratory system of gastropods">Respiratory system of gastropods</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="503" data-file-width="1148" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/220px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/330px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/440px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Book lungs of spider (shown in pink)</div></div></div>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> have lung-like structures that serve a similar respiratory purpose as, but are not evolutionarily related to, vertebrate lungs. Some <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a>, have structures called <a href="/wiki/Book_lung" title="Book lung">book lungs</a> used for atmospheric gas exchange. Some species of spider have four pairs of book lungs but most have two pairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Scorpions have <a href="/wiki/Spiracle_(arthropods)" title="Spiracle (arthropods)">spiracles</a> on their body for the entrance of air to the book lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Coconut_crab" title="Coconut crab">coconut crab</a> is terrestrial and uses structures called <a href="/wiki/Branchiostegal_lung" title="Branchiostegal lung">branchiostegal lungs</a> to breathe air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farrelly2005_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farrelly2005-116">[116]</a></sup> They cannot swim and would drown in water, yet they possess a rudimentary set of gills. They can breathe on land and hold their breath underwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The branchiostegal lungs are seen as a developmental adaptive stage from water-living to enable land-living, or from fish to amphibian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonates" title="Pulmonates">Pulmonates</a> are mostly <a href="/wiki/Land_snail" title="Land snail">land snails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slug" title="Slug">slugs</a> that have developed a simple lung from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mantle_cavity" title="Mantle cavity">mantle cavity</a>. An externally located opening called the <a href="/wiki/Pneumostome" title="Pneumostome">pneumostome</a> allows air to be taken into the mantle cavity lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_origins">Evolutionary origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Evolutionary origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The lungs of today's terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_bladder" title="Gas bladder">gas bladders</a> of today's <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> are believed to have evolved from simple sacs, as outpocketings of the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>, that allowed early fish to gulp air under oxygen-poor conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmer_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-farmer-121">[121]</a></sup> These outpocketings first arose in the  <a href="/wiki/Osteichthyes" title="Osteichthyes">bony fish</a>. In most of the <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">ray-finned fish</a> the sacs evolved into closed off gas bladders, while a number of <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">carp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a> have retained the <a href="/wiki/Physostome" title="Physostome">physostome</a> condition with the sac being open to the esophagus. In more basal bony fish, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">gar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bichir" title="Bichir">bichir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fish</a>, the bladders have evolved to primarily function as lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmer_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-farmer-121">[121]</a></sup> The lobe-finned fish gave rise to the land-based <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>. Thus, the lungs of vertebrates are <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> to the gas bladders of fish (but not to their <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lung&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 20em;">
